class="mw-indicators mw-body-content">\n</div>\n<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Great Lakes</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">\n<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>\n<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>\n<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>\n<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the Great Lakes of North America. For the African Great Lakes, see <a href="/wiki/African_Great_Lakes" title="African Great Lakes">African Great Lakes</a>. For the region, see <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_region" title="Great Lakes region">Great Lakes region</a>. For other uses of this term, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_(disambiguation)" title="Great Lakes (disambiguation)">Great Lakes (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_Lakes_from_space_crop_labeled.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GreatLakes.A2005027.1635.250m.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/GreatLakes.A2005027.1635.250m.jpg/435px-GreatLakes.A2005027.1635.250m.jpg 1.5x, title="Moderate-resolution imaging spectroradiometer">MODIS</a> image of the Great Lakes, January 27, 2005, showing ice beginning to build up around the shores of each of the lakes, with snow on the ground.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Location_of_the_Great_Lakes_in_North_America.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1748" data-file-width="1624" decoding="async" height="312" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Location_of_the_Great_Lakes_in_North_America.jpg/290px-Location_of_the_Great_Lakes_in_North_America.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Location_of_the_Great_Lakes_in_North_America.jpg/435px-Location_of_the_Great_Lakes_in_North_America.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Location_of_the_Great_Lakes_in_North_America.jpg/580px-Location_of_the_Great_Lakes_in_North_America.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Location_of_the_Great_Lakes_in_North_America.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Location in North America</div></div></div>\n<p>The <b>Great Lakes</b> (<a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <i lang="fr">les Grands-Lacs</i>), also called the <b>Laurentian Great Lakes</b><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> and the <b>Great Lakes of North America</b>, are a series of interconnected freshwater <a href="/wiki/Lake" title="Lake">lakes</a> primarily in the upper mid-east region of <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, on the <a href="/wiki/Canada%E2%80%93United_States_border" title="Canada&#8211;United States border">Canada&#8211;United States border</a>, which connect to the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a> through the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Lawrence_River" title="Saint Lawrence River">Saint Lawrence River</a>. They consist of Lakes <a href="/wiki/Lake_Superior" title="Lake Superior">Superior</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Michigan" title="Lake Michigan">Michigan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Huron" title="Lake Huron">Huron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Erie" title="Lake Erie">Erie</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Ontario" title="Lake Ontario">Ontario</a>, although hydrologically, there are four lakes, Superior, Erie, Ontario, and <a href="/wiki/Lake_Michigan%E2%80%93Huron" title="Lake Michigan&#8211;Huron">Michigan-Huron</a>. The connected lakes form the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_Waterway" title="Great Lakes Waterway">Great Lakes Waterway</a>.\n</p><p>The Great Lakes are the largest group of freshwater lakes on Earth by total area, and second-largest by total volume, containing 21% of the world\'s surface fresh water by volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The total surface is 94,250 square miles (244,106&#160;km<sup>2</sup>), and the total volume (measured at the low water datum) is 5,439 cubic miles (22,671&#160;km<sup>3</sup>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EPAphysical_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-EPAphysical-5">[5]</a></sup> slightly less than the volume of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Baikal" title="Lake Baikal">Lake Baikal</a> (5,666&#160;cu&#160;mi or 23,615&#160;km<sup>3</sup>, 22&#8211;23% of the world\'s surface fresh water). Due to their <a href="/wiki/Sea" title="Sea">sea</a>-like characteristics (rolling waves, sustained winds, strong currents, great depths, and distant horizons) the five Great Lakes have also long been referred to as <i>inland seas</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> Lake Superior is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_world%27s_largest_lakes" title="List of world\'s largest lakes">second largest lake in the world</a> by area, and the largest freshwater lake by area. Lake Michigan is the largest lake that is entirely within one country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Great Lakes began to form at the end of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">last glacial period</a> around 14,000 years ago, as retreating ice sheets exposed the basins they had carved into the land which then filled with meltwater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CordellLightfoot2008_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-CordellLightfoot2008-11">[11]</a></sup> The lakes have been a major source for transportation, migration, trade, and fishing, serving as a habitat to a large number of aquatic species in a region with much <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a>.\n</p><p>The surrounding region is called the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_region" title="Great Lakes region">Great Lakes region</a>, which includes the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_Megalopolis" title="Great Lakes Megalopolis">Great Lakes Megalopolis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sub-basins_Great_Lakes_Basin.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="575" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="197" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Sub-basins_Great_Lakes_Basin.png/240px-Sub-basins_Great_Lakes_Basin.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Sub-basins_Great_Lakes_Basin.png/360px-Sub-basins_Great_Lakes_Basin.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Sub-basins_Great_Lakes_Basin.png/480px-Sub-basins_Great_Lakes_Basin.png 2x" width="240"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sub-basins_Great_Lakes_Basin.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map of the Great Lakes Basin showing the five sub-basins within. Left to right they are: <a href="/wiki/Lake_Superior" title="Lake Superior">Superior</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Lake_Nipigon" title="Lake Nipigon">Nipigon</a>\'s basin, (magenta); <a href="/wiki/Lake_Michigan" title="Lake Michigan">Michigan</a> (cyan); <a href="/wiki/Lake_Huron" title="Lake Huron">Huron</a> (pale green); <a href="/wiki/Lake_Erie" title="Lake Erie">Erie</a> (yellow); <a href="/wiki/Lake_Ontario" title="Lake Ontario">Ontario</a> (light coral).</div></div></div>\n<p>Though the five lakes lie in separate basins, they form a single, naturally interconnected body of fresh water, within the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_Basin" title="Great Lakes Basin">Great Lakes Basin</a>. They form a chain connecting the east-central interior of North America to the Atlantic Ocean. From the interior to the outlet at the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Lawrence_River" title="Saint Lawrence River">Saint Lawrence River</a>, water flows from Superior to Huron and Michigan, southward to Erie, and finally northward to Lake Ontario. The lakes drain a large watershed via many rivers, and are studded with approximately 35,000 islands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bennett1999as_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bennett1999as-13">[13]</a></sup> There are also several thousand smaller lakes, often called "inland lakes," within the basin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Likens2010at_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Likens2010at-14">[14]</a></sup> The surface area of the five primary lakes combined is roughly equal to the size of the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, while the surface area of the entire basin (the lakes and the land they drain) is about the size of the UK and France combined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grady_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grady-15">[15]</a></sup> Lake Michigan is the only one of the Great Lakes that is entirely within the United States; the others form a water boundary between the United States and Canada. The lakes are divided among the jurisdictions of the Canadian province of <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a> and the U.S. states of <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minnesota" title="Minnesota">Minnesota</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indiana" title="Indiana">Indiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>, and <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>. Both Ontario and Michigan include in their boundaries portions of four of the lakes: Ontario does not border Lake Michigan, and Michigan does not border Lake Ontario. New York and Wisconsin\'s jurisdictions extend into two lakes, and each of the remaining states into one of the lakes.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bathymetry">Bathymetry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Bathymetry">edit</a><span class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_Lakes_bathymetry_map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="731" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="286" class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Great_Lakes_bathymetry_map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Great Lakes <a href="/wiki/Bathymetry" title="Bathymetry">bathymetry</a>. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GLBathEr_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-GLBathEr-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GLBathHur_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-GLBathHur-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GLBathMich_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-GLBathMich-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GLBathOnt_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-GLBathOnt-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GLBathSup_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-GLBathSup-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GLOBE_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-GLOBE-24">[24]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>As the surfaces of Lakes Superior, Huron, Michigan, and Erie are all approximately the same elevation above sea level, while Lake Ontario is significantly lower, and because the <a href="/wiki/Niagara_Escarpment" title="Niagara Escarpment">Niagara Escarpment</a> precludes all natural navigation, the four upper lakes are commonly called the "upper great lakes". This designation, however, is not universal. Those living on the shore of Lake Superior often refer to all the other lakes as "the lower lakes", because they are farther south. Sailors of bulk freighters transferring cargoes from Lake Superior and northern Lake Michigan and Lake Huron to ports on Lake Erie or Ontario commonly refer to the latter as the lower lakes and Lakes Michigan, Huron, and Superior as the upper lakes. This corresponds to thinking of Lakes Erie and Ontario as "down south" and the others as "up north". Vessels sailing north on Lake Michigan are considered "upbound" even though they are sailing toward its effluent current.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bowlus2010_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bowlus2010-25">[25]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Primary_connecting_waterways">Primary connecting waterways</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Primary connecting waterways">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/2009-09-18_3060x2040_chicago_skyline.jpg/330px-2009-09-18_3060x2040_chicago_skyline.jpg 1.5x, class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2009-09-18_3060x2040_chicago_skyline.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a> on Lake Michigan is in the western part of the lakes megalopolis, and the site of the waterway linking the lakes to the Mississippi River valley</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DowntownDetroit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" href="/wiki/File:DowntownDetroit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Detroit" title="Detroit">Detroit</a> on the Detroit River links the region\'s central metropolitan areas.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skyline_of_Toronto_viewed_from_Harbour.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1800" decoding="async" height="147" class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skyline_of_Toronto_viewed_from_Harbour.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a> on Lake Ontario is in the eastern section of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_Megalopolis" title="Great Lakes Megalopolis">Great Lakes Megalopolis</a></div></div></div>\n<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Chicago_River" title="Chicago River">Chicago River</a> and <a href="/wiki/Calumet_River" title="Calumet River">Calumet River</a> systems connect the Great Lakes Basin to the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a> System through man-made alterations and canals.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/St._Marys_River_(Michigan%E2%80%93Ontario)" title="St. Marys River (Michigan&#8211;Ontario)">St. Marys River</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Soo_Locks" title="Soo Locks">Soo Locks</a>, connects Lake Superior to Lake Huron.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Straits_of_Mackinac" title="Straits of Mackinac">Straits of Mackinac</a> connect Lake Michigan to Lake Huron (which are hydrologically one).</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/St._Clair_River" title="St. Clair River">St. Clair River</a> connects Lake Huron to <a href="/wiki/Lake_St._Clair" title="Lake St. Clair">Lake St. Clair</a>.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Detroit_River" title="Detroit River">Detroit River</a> connects Lake St. Clair to Lake Erie.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Niagara_River" title="Niagara River">Niagara River</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Niagara_Falls" title="Niagara Falls">Niagara Falls</a>, connects Lake Erie to Lake Ontario.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Welland_Canal" title="Welland Canal">Welland Canal</a>, bypassing the Falls, connects Lake Erie to Lake Ontario.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Saint_Lawrence_River" title="Saint Lawrence River">Saint Lawrence River</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Lawrence_Seaway" title="Saint Lawrence Seaway">Saint Lawrence Seaway</a> connect Lake Ontario to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gulf_of_St._Lawrence" title="Gulf of St. Lawrence">Gulf of St. Lawrence</a>, which connects to the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic Ocean</a>.</li></ul>\n<h3><span id="Lake_Michigan.E2.80.93Huron"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Lake_Michigan&#8211;Huron">Lake Michigan&#8211;Huron</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Lake Michigan&#8211;Huron">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lake_Michigan%E2%80%93Huron" title="Lake Michigan&#8211;Huron">Lake Michigan&#8211;Huron</a></div>\n<p>Lakes Huron and Michigan are sometimes considered a single lake, called Lake Michigan&#8211;Huron, because they are one hydrological body of water connected by the <a href="/wiki/Straits_of_Mackinac" title="Straits of Mackinac">Straits of Mackinac</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The straits are five miles (8&#160;km) wide<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grady_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Grady-15">[15]</a></sup> and 120 feet (37&#160;m) deep; the water levels &#8211; <span class="cleanup-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">currently</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (January 2019)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> at 577 feet (176&#160;m) &#8211; rise and fall together,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-27">[27]</a></sup> and the flow between Michigan and Huron frequently reverses direction.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_significant_bodies_of_water">Other significant bodies of water</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Other significant bodies of water">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Lake_Nipigon" title="Lake Nipigon">Lake Nipigon</a>, connected to Lake Superior by the <a href="/wiki/Nipigon_River" title="Nipigon River">Nipigon River</a>, is surrounded by <a href="/wiki/Layered_intrusion" title="Layered intrusion">sill-like formations</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mafic" title="Mafic">mafic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ultramafic_rock" title="Ultramafic rock">ultramafic</a> <a href="/wiki/Igneous_rock" title="Igneous rock">igneous rock</a> hundreds of meters high. The lake lies in the <a href="/wiki/Nipigon_Embayment" title="Nipigon Embayment">Nipigon Embayment</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Aulacogen" title="Aulacogen">failed arm</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Triple_junction" title="Triple junction">triple junction</a> (centered beneath Lake Superior) in the <a href="/wiki/Midcontinent_Rift_System" title="Midcontinent Rift System">Midcontinent Rift System</a> event, estimated at 1,109 million years ago.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Green_Bay_(Lake_Michigan)" title="Green Bay (Lake Michigan)">Green Bay</a> is an arm of Lake Michigan, along the south coast of the <a href="/wiki/Upper_Peninsula_of_Michigan" title="Upper Peninsula of Michigan">Upper Peninsula of Michigan</a> and the east coast of Wisconsin. It is separated from the rest of the lake by the <a href="/wiki/Door_Peninsula" title="Door Peninsula">Door Peninsula</a> in Wisconsin, the <a href="/wiki/Garden_Peninsula" title="Garden Peninsula">Garden Peninsula</a> in Michigan, and the chain of islands between them, all of which were formed by the <a href="/wiki/Niagara_Escarpment" title="Niagara Escarpment">Niagara Escarpment</a>.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Lake_Winnebago" title="Lake Winnebago">Lake Winnebago</a>, connected to Green Bay by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fox_River_(Wisconsin)" title="Fox River (Wisconsin)">Fox River</a>, serves as part of the <a href="/wiki/Fox%E2%80%93Wisconsin_Waterway" title="Fox&#8211;Wisconsin Waterway">Fox&#8211;Wisconsin Waterway</a> and is part of a larger system of lakes in Wisconsin known as the <a href="/wiki/Winnebago_Pool" title="Winnebago Pool">Winnebago Pool</a>.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Grand_Traverse_Bay" title="Grand Traverse Bay">Grand Traverse Bay</a> is an arm of Lake Michigan on Michigan\'s west coast, being one of the largest natural harbors in the Great Lakes. The bay has one large peninsula<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> and one major island known as Power Island. Its name is derived from Jacques Marquette\'s crossing of the bay from <a href="/wiki/Norwood,_Michigan" title="Norwood, Michigan">Norwood</a> to <a href="/wiki/Northport,_Michigan" title="Northport, Michigan">Northport</a> which he called <i>La Grande Traversee</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Georgian_Bay" title="Georgian Bay">Georgian Bay</a> is an arm of Lake Huron, extending northeast from the lake entirely within Ontario. The bay, along with its narrow westerly extensions of the <a href="/wiki/North_Channel_(Ontario)" title="North Channel (Ontario)">North Channel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mississagi_Strait" title="Mississagi Strait">Mississagi Strait</a>, is separated from the rest of the lake by the <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Peninsula" title="Bruce Peninsula">Bruce Peninsula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manitoulin_Island" title="Manitoulin Island">Manitoulin Island</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cockburn_Island_(Ontario)" title="Cockburn Island (Ontario)">Cockburn Island</a>, all of which were also formed by the Niagara Escarpment.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Lake_Nipissing" title="Lake Nipissing">Lake Nipissing</a>, connected to Georgian Bay by the <a href="/wiki/French_River_(Ontario)" title="French River (Ontario)">French River</a>, contains two <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_pipe" title="Volcanic pipe">volcanic pipes</a>, which are the <a href="/wiki/Manitou_Islands_(Lake_Nipissing)" title="Manitou Islands (Lake Nipissing)">Manitou Islands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Callander_Bay" title="Callander Bay">Callander Bay</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> These pipes were formed by a violent, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Supersonic" title="Supersonic">supersonic</a> eruption of deep-origin. The lake lies in the <a href="/wiki/Ottawa-Bonnechere_Graben" title="Ottawa-Bonnechere Graben">Ottawa-Bonnechere Graben</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Mesozoic" title="Mesozoic">Mesozoic</a> <a href="/wiki/Rift_valley" title="Rift valley">rift valley</a> that formed 175 million years ago.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Lake_Simcoe" title="Lake Simcoe">Lake Simcoe</a>, connected to Georgian Bay by the <a href="/wiki/Severn_River_(central_Ontario)" title="Severn River (central Ontario)">Severn River</a>, serves as part of the <a href="/wiki/Trent%E2%80%93Severn_Waterway" title="Trent&#8211;Severn Waterway">Trent&#8211;Severn Waterway</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Canal" title="Canal">canal</a> route traversing <a href="/wiki/Southern_Ontario" title="Southern Ontario">Southern Ontario</a> between Lakes Ontario and Huron.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Lake_St._Clair" title="Lake St. Clair">Lake St. Clair</a>, connected with Lake Huron to its north by the <a href="/wiki/St._Clair_River" title="St. Clair River">St. Clair River</a> and with Lake Erie to its south by the <a href="/wiki/Detroit_River" title="Detroit River">Detroit River</a>. Although it is 17 times smaller in area than Lake Ontario and only rarely included in the listings of the Great Lakes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LakeStClair_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-LakeStClair-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-U.S.Army_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-U.S.Army-32">[32]</a></sup> proposals for its official recognition as a Great Lake are occasionally made, which would affect its inclusion in scientific <a href="/wiki/Research" title="Research">research</a> projects, etc., designated as related to "The Great Lakes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Islands">Islands</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Islands">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Put-in-Bay_Airport.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Put-in-Bay_Airport.jpg/220px-Put-in-Bay_Airport.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Put-in-Bay_Airport.jpg/330px-Put-in-Bay_Airport.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Put-in-Bay_Airport.jpg/440px-Put-in-Bay_Airport.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Put-in-Bay_Airport.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/South_Bass_Island" title="South Bass Island">South Bass Island</a> in Lake Erie</div></div></div>\n<p>Dispersed throughout the Great Lakes are approximately <a href="/wiki/List_of_islands_of_the_Great_Lakes" title="List of islands of the Great Lakes">35,000 islands</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bennett1999as_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bennett1999as-13">[13]</a></sup> The largest among them is <a href="/wiki/Manitoulin_Island" title="Manitoulin Island">Manitoulin Island</a> in Lake Huron, the largest island in any inland body of water in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dunn1996bv_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dunn1996bv-34">[34]</a></sup> The second-largest island is <a href="/wiki/Isle_Royale" title="Isle Royale">Isle Royale</a> in Lake Superior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huber(U.S.)1975_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Huber(U.S.)1975-35">[35]</a></sup> Both of these islands are large enough to contain multiple lakes themselves&#8212;for instance, Manitoulin Island\'s <a href="/wiki/Lake_Manitou" title="Lake Manitou">Lake Manitou</a> is the world\'s largest lake on a freshwater island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manivanan2008_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Manivanan2008-36">[36]</a></sup> Some of these lakes even have their own islands, like <a class="external text" href="https://www.google.com/maps/place/Treasure+Island/@45.7842067,-82.3090954,11.01z/data=!4m5!3m4!1s0x4d3210e9a27b7ee5:0x3765f7b2af26e702!8m2!3d45.7635997!4d-82.1780099" rel="nofollow">Treasure Island</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lake_Mindemoya" title="Lake Mindemoya">Lake Mindemoya</a> in <a href="/wiki/Manitoulin_Island" title="Manitoulin Island">Manitoulin Island</a>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Peninsulas">Peninsulas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Peninsulas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Upper_Peninsula_of_Michigan" title="Upper Peninsula of Michigan">Upper Peninsula of Michigan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lower_Peninsula_of_Michigan" title="Lower Peninsula of Michigan">Lower Peninsula of Michigan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ontario_Peninsula" title="Ontario Peninsula">Ontario Peninsula</a></div>\n<p>The Great Lakes also have several peninsulas between them, including <a href="/wiki/Door_Peninsula" title="Door Peninsula">the Door Peninsula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Peninsulas_of_Michigan" title="Peninsulas of Michigan">the Peninsulas of Michigan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ontario_Peninsula" title="Ontario Peninsula">the Ontario Peninsula</a>. Some of these peninsulas even contain smaller peninsulas, like <a href="/wiki/Keweenaw_Peninsula" title="Keweenaw Peninsula">the Keweenaw Peninsula</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Thumb" title="The Thumb">the Thumb Peninsula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bruce_Peninsula" title="Bruce Peninsula">the Bruce Peninsula</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Niagara_Peninsula" title="Niagara Peninsula">the Niagara Peninsula</a>. Population centers on the peninsulas include <a href="/wiki/Grand_Rapids,_Michigan" title="Grand Rapids, Michigan">Grand Rapids, Michigan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Detroit,_Michigan" title="Detroit, Michigan">Detroit, Michigan</a>, <a href="/wiki/London,_Ontario" title="London, Ontario">London, Ontario</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hamilton,_Ontario" title="Hamilton, Ontario">Hamilton, Ontario</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toronto,_Ontario" title="Toronto, Ontario">Toronto, Ontario</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shipping_connection_to_the_ocean">Shipping connection to the ocean</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Shipping connection to the ocean">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Saint_Lawrence_Seaway" title="Saint Lawrence Seaway">Saint Lawrence Seaway</a> and <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_Waterway" title="Great Lakes Waterway">Great Lakes Waterway</a> make the Great Lakes accessible to ocean-going vessels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McCalla1994_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-McCalla1994-37">[37]</a></sup> However, shifts in shipping to wider ocean-going <a href="/wiki/Container_ship" title="Container ship">container ships</a>&#8212;which do not fit through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canal_lock" title="Canal lock">locks</a> on these routes&#8212;have limited container shipping on the lakes. Most Great Lakes trade is of bulk material, and bulk freighters of <a href="/wiki/Seawaymax" title="Seawaymax">Seawaymax</a>-size or less can move throughout the entire lakes and out to the Atlantic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coastal_Sediments_\'07_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coastal_Sediments_\'07-38">[38]</a></sup> Larger ships are confined to working in the lakes themselves. Only barges can access the <a href="/wiki/Illinois_Waterway" title="Illinois Waterway">Illinois Waterway</a> system providing access to the Gulf of Mexico via the Mississippi River. Despite their vast size, large sections of the Great Lakes freeze over in winter, interrupting most shipping from January to March. Some <a href="/wiki/Icebreaker" title="Icebreaker">icebreakers</a> ply the lakes, keeping the shipping lanes open through other periods of ice on the lakes.\n</p><p>The Great Lakes are also connected by the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Sanitary_and_Ship_Canal" title="Chicago Sanitary and Ship Canal">Chicago Sanitary and Ship Canal</a> to the Gulf of Mexico by way of the <a href="/wiki/Illinois_River" title="Illinois River">Illinois River</a> (from the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_River" title="Chicago River">Chicago River</a>) and the Mississippi River. An alternate track is via the Illinois River (from Chicago), to the Mississippi, up the Ohio, and then through the <a href="/wiki/Tennessee%E2%80%93Tombigbee_Waterway" title="Tennessee&#8211;Tombigbee Waterway">Tennessee&#8211;Tombigbee Waterway</a> (a combination of a series of rivers and lakes and canals), to <a href="/wiki/Mobile_Bay" title="Mobile Bay">Mobile Bay</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a>. Commercial tug-and-barge traffic on these waterways is heavy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Census1908_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Census1908-39">[39]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Pleasure boats can also enter or exit the Great Lakes by way of the <a href="/wiki/Erie_Canal" title="Erie Canal">Erie Canal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hudson_River" title="Hudson River">Hudson River</a> in New York. The Erie Canal connects to the Great Lakes at the east end of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Erie" title="Lake Erie">Lake Erie</a> (at <a href="/wiki/Buffalo,_New_York" title="Buffalo, New York">Buffalo, New York</a>) and at the south side of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Ontario" title="Lake Ontario">Lake Ontario</a> (at <a href="/wiki/Oswego,_New_York" title="Oswego, New York">Oswego, New York</a>).\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water_levels">Water levels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Water levels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 2009, the lakes contained 84% of the surface freshwater of North America;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-epa-40">[40]</a></sup> if the water were evenly distributed over the entire continent\'s land area, it would reach a depth of 1.5 meters (5 feet).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grady_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-Grady-15">[15]</a></sup> The source of water levels in the lakes is tied to what was left by melting glaciers when the lakes took their present form. Annually, only about 1% is "new" water originating from rivers, precipitation, and groundwater springs that drain into the lakes. Historically, evaporation has been balanced by drainage, making the level of the lakes constant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grady_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-Grady-15">[15]</a></sup> While the lake levels have been preserved, intensive human population growth only began in the region in the 20th century and continues today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grady_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-Grady-15">[15]</a></sup> At least two human water use activities have been identified as having the potential to affect the lakes\' levels: diversion (the transfer of water to other watersheds) and consumption (substantially done today by the use of lake water to power and cool electric generation plants, resulting in evaporation).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The water level of Lake Michigan&#8211;Huron had remained fairly constant over the 20th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BolsengaHerdendorf1993_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-BolsengaHerdendorf1993-42">[42]</a></sup> but has nevertheless dropped more than 6 feet from the record high in 1986 to the low of 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jsonline_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-jsonline-43">[43]</a></sup> One newspaper reported that the long-term average level has gone down about 20 inches because of dredging and subsequent erosion in the <a href="/wiki/St._Clair_River" title="St. Clair River">St. Clair River</a>. Lake Michigan&#8211;Huron hit all-time record low levels in 2013; according to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Army_Corps_of_Engineers" title="US Army Corps of Engineers">US Army Corps of Engineers</a>, the previous record low had been set in 1964.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jsonline_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-jsonline-43">[43]</a></sup> By April 2015 the water level had recovered to 7 inches (17.5&#160;cm) more than the "long term monthly average".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name_origins">Name origins</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Name origins">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_Lakes_from_space_during_early_spring.webp"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1400" data-file-width="2100" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Great_Lakes_from_space_during_early_spring.webp/220px-Great_Lakes_from_space_during_early_spring.webp.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Great_Lakes_from_space_during_early_spring.webp/330px-Great_Lakes_from_space_during_early_spring.webp.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Great_Lakes_from_space_during_early_spring.webp/440px-Great_Lakes_from_space_during_early_spring.webp.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Great_Lakes_from_space_during_early_spring.webp" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Great Lakes during early spring</div></div></div>\n<dl><dt>Lake Erie</dt>\n<dd>From the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Erie_(tribe)" title="Erie (tribe)">Erie tribe</a>, a shortened form of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iroquoian" title="Iroquoian">Iroquoian</a> word <i><i lang="iro" title="Iroquoian languages collective text">erielhonan</i></i> "long tail".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Room2006_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Room2006-45">[45]</a></sup></dd>\n<dt>Lake Huron</dt>\n<dd>The native <a href="/wiki/Wyandot_people" title="Wyandot people">Wyandot</a> originally referred to the lake by the name <i><i lang="wya" title="Wyandot language text">karegnondi</i></i>, a word which has been variously translated as "Freshwater Sea", "Lake of the <a href="/wiki/Wyandot_people" title="Wyandot people">Hurons</a>", or simply "lake".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fj6_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-fj6-47">[47]</a></sup> The first French explorers in the area named the lake after the inhabitants of the area, the <a href="/wiki/Wyandot_people" title="Wyandot people">Wyandot</a> or "Hurons".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Room2006b_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Room2006b-48">[48]</a></sup></dd>\n<dt>Lake Michigan</dt>\n<dd>From the <a href="/wiki/Ojibwe_language" title="Ojibwe language">Ojibwa</a> word <i><i lang="oj" title="Ojibwe language text">mishi-gami</i></i> "great water" or "large lake".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WeilandWilsey2010_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-WeilandWilsey2010-49">[49]</a></sup></dd>\n<dt>Lake Ontario</dt>\n<dd>From the <a href="/wiki/Wyandot_language" title="Wyandot language">Wyandot</a> (Huron) word <i><i lang="wya" title="Wyandot language text">ontar&#237;\'io</i></i> "lake of shining waters".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ylvisaker2004_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ylvisaker2004-50">[50]</a></sup></dd>\n<dt>Lake Superior</dt>\n<dd>English translation of the French term <i><i lang="fr" title="French language text">lac sup&#233;rieur</i></i> "upper lake", referring to its position north of Lake Huron. The indigenous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ojibwe_people" title="Ojibwe people">Ojibwe</a> call it <i><i lang="oj" title="Ojibwe language text">gichi-gami</i></i> (from <a href="/wiki/Ojibwe_language" title="Ojibwe language">Ojibwe</a> <i><i lang="oj" title="Ojibwe language text">gichi</i></i> "big, large, great"; <i><i lang="oj" title="Ojibwe language text">gami</i></i> "water, lake, sea"). Popularized in French-influenced transliteration as <i>Gitchigumi</i> as in <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Lightfoot" title="Gordon Lightfoot">Gordon Lightfoot</a>\'s 1976 story song, <a href="/wiki/The_Wreck_of_the_Edmund_Fitzgerald" title="The Wreck of the Edmund Fitzgerald"><i>The Wreck of the</i> Edmund Fitzgerald</a>, or <i>Gitchee Gumee</i> as in <a href="/wiki/Henry_Wadsworth_Longfellow" title="Henry Wadsworth Longfellow">Henry Wadsworth Longfellow</a>\'s 1855 epic poem, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Song_of_Hiawatha" title="The Song of Hiawatha">The Song of Hiawatha</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dep_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-dep-17">[17]</a></sup></dd></dl>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Statistics">Statistics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Statistics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The Great Lakes contain 21% of the world\'s surface fresh water: 5,472 cubic miles (22,810&#160;km<sup>3</sup>), or 6.0&#215;10<sup>15</sup> U.S.&#160;gallons, that is 6 quadrillion U.S gallons, (2.3&#215;10<sup>16</sup> liters). This is enough water to cover the 48 contiguous U.S. states to a uniform depth of 9.5 feet (2.9&#160;m). Although the lakes contain a large percentage of the world\'s fresh water, the Great Lakes supply only a small portion of U.S. drinking water on a national basis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CaytonSisson2006_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-CaytonSisson2006-51">[51]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The total <a href="/wiki/Surface_area" title="Surface area">surface area</a> of the lakes is approximately 94,250 square miles (244,100&#160;km<sup>2</sup>)&#8212;nearly the same size as the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, and larger than the U.S. states of <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rhode_Island" title="Rhode Island">Rhode Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts" title="Massachusetts">Massachusetts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vermont" title="Vermont">Vermont</a>, and <a href="/wiki/New_Hampshire" title="New Hampshire">New Hampshire</a> combined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor/Schechter/Wolfson_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor/Schechter/Wolfson-52">[52]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Great Lakes <a href="/wiki/Coast" title="Coast">coast</a> measures approximately 10,500 miles (16,900&#160;km);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grady_15-6"><a href="#cite_note-Grady-15">[15]</a></sup> however, the length of a coastline is impossible to measure exactly and is not a well-defined measure (see <a href="/wiki/Coastline_paradox" title="Coastline paradox">Coastline paradox</a>). Of the total 10,500 miles (16,900&#160;km) of shoreline, Canada borders approximately 5,200 miles (8,400&#160;km), while the remaining 5,300 miles (8,500&#160;km) are bordered by the United States. <a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a> has the longest shoreline of the United States, bordering roughly 3,288 miles (5,292&#160;km) of shoreline, followed by <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a> (820 miles (1,320&#160;km)), <a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York</a> (473 miles (761&#160;km)), and <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a> (312 miles (502&#160;km)).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Traversing the shoreline of all the lakes would cover a distance roughly equivalent to travelling half-way around the world at the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grady_15-7"><a href="#cite_note-Grady-15">[15]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glacial_lakes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="545" data-file-width="750" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Glacial_lakes.jpg/300px-Glacial_lakes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Glacial_lakes.jpg/450px-Glacial_lakes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Glacial_lakes.jpg/600px-Glacial_lakes.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glacial_lakes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A diagram of the formation of the Great Lakes</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plate_21_-_Glacial_Lake_Algonquin_and_its_Correlatives_(USGS_1915).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="852" data-file-width="1094" decoding="async" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Plate_21_-_Glacial_Lake_Algonquin_and_its_Correlatives_%28USGS_1915%29.JPG/300px-Plate_21_-_Glacial_Lake_Algonquin_and_its_Correlatives_%28USGS_1915%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Plate_21_-_Glacial_Lake_Algonquin_and_its_Correlatives_%28USGS_1915%29.JPG/450px-Plate_21_-_Glacial_Lake_Algonquin_and_its_Correlatives_%28USGS_1915%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Plate_21_-_Glacial_Lake_Algonquin_and_its_Correlatives_%28USGS_1915%29.JPG/600px-Plate_21_-_Glacial_Lake_Algonquin_and_its_Correlatives_%28USGS_1915%29.JPG 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plate_21_-_Glacial_Lake_Algonquin_and_its_Correlatives_(USGS_1915).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of Glacial Lake Algonquin and its Correlatives (USGS 1915)</div></div></div>\n<p>It has been estimated that the foundational geology that created the conditions shaping the present day upper Great Lakes was laid from 1.1 to 1.2 billion years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grady_15-8"><a href="#cite_note-Grady-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schmus_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schmus-54">[54]</a></sup> when two previously fused <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tectonic_plates" title="Tectonic plates">tectonic plates</a> split apart and created the <a href="/wiki/Midcontinent_Rift_System" title="Midcontinent Rift System">Midcontinent Rift</a>, which crossed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_Tectonic_Zone" title="Great Lakes Tectonic Zone">Great Lakes Tectonic Zone</a>. A valley was formed providing a basin that eventually became modern day Lake Superior. When a second fault line, the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Lawrence_rift_system" title="Saint Lawrence rift system">Saint Lawrence rift</a>, formed approximately 570&#160;million years ago,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grady_15-9"><a href="#cite_note-Grady-15">[15]</a></sup> the basis for Lakes Ontario and Erie were created, along with what would become the Saint Lawrence River.\n</p><p>The Great Lakes are estimated to have been formed at the end of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Last_glacial_period" title="Last glacial period">last glacial period</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_glaciation" title="Wisconsin glaciation">Wisconsin glaciation</a> ended 10,000 to 12,000 years ago), when the <a href="/wiki/Laurentide_Ice_Sheet" title="Laurentide Ice Sheet">Laurentide Ice Sheet</a> receded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CordellLightfoot2008_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-CordellLightfoot2008-11">[11]</a></sup> The retreat of the ice sheet left behind a large amount of meltwater (see <a href="/wiki/Lake_Algonquin" title="Lake Algonquin">Lake Algonquin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lake_Chicago" title="Lake Chicago">Lake Chicago</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glacial_Lake_Iroquois" title="Glacial Lake Iroquois">Glacial Lake Iroquois</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Champlain_Sea" title="Champlain Sea">Champlain Sea</a>) that filled up the basins that the glaciers had carved, thus creating the Great Lakes as we know them today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Because of the uneven nature of glacier <a href="/wiki/Erosion" title="Erosion">erosion</a>, some higher hills became <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islands_of_the_Great_Lakes" title="Islands of the Great Lakes">Great Lakes islands</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Niagara_Escarpment" title="Niagara Escarpment">Niagara Escarpment</a> follows the contour of the Great Lakes between New York and Wisconsin. Land below the glaciers <a href="/wiki/Post-glacial_rebound" title="Post-glacial rebound">"rebounded"</a> as it was uncovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Since the glaciers covered some areas longer than others, this glacial rebound occurred at different rates.\n</p><p>A notable modern phenomenon is the formation of <a href="/wiki/Ice_volcano" title="Ice volcano">ice volcanoes</a> over the lakes during wintertime. Storm-generated waves carve the lakes\' ice sheet and create conical mounds through the eruption of water and slush. The process is only well-documented in the Great Lakes, and has been credited with sparing the southern shorelines from worse rocky erosion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fahnestock1973_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fahnestock1973-57">[57]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The Great Lakes have a humid continental climate,\n<a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="K&#246;ppen climate classification">K&#246;ppen climate classification</a> <b>Dfa</b> (in southern areas) and <b>Dfb</b> (in northern parts)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa-atlas-ch2_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-epa-atlas-ch2-58">[58]</a></sup> with varying influences from air masses from other regions including dry, cold Arctic systems, mild Pacific air masses from the West, and warm, wet tropical systems from the south and the Gulf of Mexico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-king_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-king-59">[59]</a></sup> The lakes themselves also have a moderating effect on the climate; they can also increase precipitation totals and produce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_effect_snow" title="Lake effect snow">lake effect snowfall</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epa-atlas-ch2_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-epa-atlas-ch2-58">[58]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lake_effect">Lake effect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Lake effect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lake-effect_snow" title="Lake-effect snow">Lake-effect snow</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lake_effect_snow_wind_direction_bands1.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3845" data-file-width="1436" decoding="async" height="589" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Lake_effect_snow_wind_direction_bands1.png/220px-Lake_effect_snow_wind_direction_bands1.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Lake_effect_snow_wind_direction_bands1.png/330px-Lake_effect_snow_wind_direction_bands1.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Lake_effect_snow_wind_direction_bands1.png/440px-Lake_effect_snow_wind_direction_bands1.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lake_effect_snow_wind_direction_bands1.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The location of common lake effect bands on the Great Lakes</div></div></div>\n<p>The most well-known winter effect of the Great Lakes on regional weather is the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_effect_snow" title="Lake effect snow">lake effect</a></i> in snowfall, which is sometimes very localized. Even late in winter, the lakes often have no icepack in the middle. The prevailing winds from the west pick up the air and moisture from the lake surface, which is slightly warmer in relation to the cold surface winds above. As the slightly warmer, moist air passes over the colder land surface, the moisture often produces concentrated, heavy snowfall that sets up in bands or "streamers". This is similar to the effect of warmer air dropping snow as it passes over mountain ranges. During freezing weather with high winds, the "snow belts" receive regular snow fall from this localized weather pattern, especially along the eastern shores of the lakes. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Snow_belt" title="Snow belt">Snow belts</a> are found in Wisconsin, Michigan, Ohio, Pennsylvania, and New York, United States; and Ontario, Canada.\n</p><p>The lakes also moderate seasonal temperatures to some degree, but not with as large an influence as do large oceans; they absorb heat and cool the air in summer, then slowly radiate that heat in autumn. They protect against frost during transitional weather, and keep the summertime temperatures cooler than further inland. This effect can be very localized and overridden by offshore wind patterns. This temperature buffering produces areas known as "<a href="/wiki/Fruit_Belt" title="Fruit Belt">fruit belts</a>", where fruit can be produced that is typically grown much farther south. For instance, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Michigan" title="Western Michigan">Western Michigan</a> has apple and cherry orchards, and vineyards cultivated adjacent to the lake shore as far north as the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Traverse_Bay" title="Grand Traverse Bay">Grand Traverse Bay</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nottawasaga_Bay" title="Nottawasaga Bay">Nottawasaga Bay</a> in central Ontario. The eastern shore of Lake Michigan and the southern shore of Lake Erie have many successful wineries because of the moderating effect, as does the <a href="/wiki/Niagara_Peninsula" title="Niagara Peninsula">Niagara Peninsula</a> between Lake Erie and Lake Ontario. A similar phenomenon allows wineries to flourish in the <a href="/wiki/Finger_Lakes" title="Finger Lakes">Finger Lakes</a> region of New York, as well as in <a href="/wiki/Prince_Edward_County,_Ontario" title="Prince Edward County, Ontario">Prince Edward County, Ontario</a> on Lake Ontario\'s northeast shore. Related to the lake effect is the regular occurrence of <a href="/wiki/Fog" title="Fog">fog</a> over medium-sized areas, particularly along the shorelines of the lakes. This is most noticeable along Lake Superior\'s shores.\n</p><p>The Great Lakes have been observed to help intensify storms, such as <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Hazel" title="Hurricane Hazel">Hurricane Hazel</a> in 1954, and the <a href="/wiki/2011_Goderich,_Ontario_tornado" title="2011 Goderich, Ontario tornado">2011 Goderich, Ontario tornado</a>, which moved onshore as a tornadic waterspout. In 1996 a rare tropical or subtropical storm was observed forming in Lake Huron, dubbed the <a href="/wiki/1996_Lake_Huron_cyclone" title="1996 Lake Huron cyclone">1996 Lake Huron cyclone</a>. Rather large severe thunderstorms covering wide areas are well known in the Great Lakes during mid-summer; these <a href="/wiki/Mesoscale_convective_complex" title="Mesoscale convective complex">Mesoscale convective complexes</a> or MCCs<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> can cause damage to wide swaths of forest and shatter glass in city buildings. These storms mainly occur during the night, and the systems sometimes have small embedded tornadoes, but more often straight-line winds accompanied by intense lightning.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">Ecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:USGS_image_cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="759" data-file-width="1073" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/USGS_image_cropped.jpg/300px-USGS_image_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/USGS_image_cropped.jpg/450px-USGS_image_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/USGS_image_cropped.jpg/600px-USGS_image_cropped.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:USGS_image_cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Generalized schematic of Great Lakes waterline ecosystem</div></div></div>\n<p>Historically, the Great Lakes, in addition to their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_ecology" title="Lake ecology">lake ecology</a>, were surrounded by various <a href="/wiki/Forest" title="Forest">forest</a> <a href="/wiki/Ecoregion" title="Ecoregion">ecoregions</a> (except in a relatively small area of southeast Lake Michigan where savanna or prairie occasionally intruded). Logging, urbanization, and agriculture uses have changed that relationship. In the early 21st century, Lake Superior\'s shores are 91% forested, Lake Huron 68%, Lake Ontario 49%, Lake Michigan 41%, and Lake Erie, where logging and urbanization has been most extensive, 21%. Some of these forests are second or third growth (i.e. they have been logged before, changing their composition). At least 13 wildlife species are documented as becoming extinct since the arrival of Europeans, and many more are threatened or endangered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grady_15-10"><a href="#cite_note-Grady-15">[15]</a></sup> Meanwhile, exotic and invasive species have also been introduced.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fauna">Fauna</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Fauna">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Fauna_of_the_Great_Lakes_region_(North_America)" title="Category:Fauna of the Great Lakes region (North America)">Category:Fauna of the Great Lakes region (North America)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Category:Fish_of_the_Great_Lakes" title="Category:Fish of the Great Lakes">Category:Fish of the Great Lakes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Asian_carp_in_North_America" title="Asian carp in North America">Asian carp in North America</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lakesturgeon_public_U.S.Fish%26Wildlife.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="1050" decoding="async" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Lakesturgeon_public_U.S.Fish%26Wildlife.jpg/220px-Lakesturgeon_public_U.S.Fish%26Wildlife.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Lakesturgeon_public_U.S.Fish%26Wildlife.jpg/330px-Lakesturgeon_public_U.S.Fish%26Wildlife.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Lakesturgeon_public_U.S.Fish%26Wildlife.jpg/440px-Lakesturgeon_public_U.S.Fish%26Wildlife.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lakesturgeon_public_U.S.Fish%26Wildlife.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lake_sturgeon" title="Lake sturgeon">Lake sturgeon</a>, the largest native fish in the Great Lakes and the subject of extensive commercial fishing in the 19th and 20th centuries is listed as a threatened species<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>The organisms living on the bottom of shallow waters are similar to those found in smaller lakes. The deep waters, however, contain organisms found only in deep, cold lakes of the northern latitudes. These include the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Delicate_opossum_shrimp&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Delicate opossum shrimp (page does not exist)">delicate opossum shrimp</a> (order <a href="/wiki/Mysida" title="Mysida">mysida</a>), the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Deepwater_scud&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Deepwater scud (page does not exist)">deepwater scud</a> (a crustacean of the order <a href="/wiki/Amphipoda" title="Amphipoda">amphipoda</a>), two types of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Copepods" title="Copepods">copepods</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Deepwater_sculpin" title="Deepwater sculpin">deepwater sculpin</a> (a spiny, large-headed fish).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Great Lakes are an important source of <a href="/wiki/Fishing" title="Fishing">fishing</a>. Early European settlers were astounded by both the variety and quantity of fish; there were 150 different species in the Great Lakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grady_15-11"><a href="#cite_note-Grady-15">[15]</a></sup> Throughout history, fish populations were the early indicator of the condition of the Lakes and have remained one of the key indicators even in the current era of sophisticated analyses and measuring instruments. According to the bi-national (U.S. and Canadian) resource book, <i>The Great Lakes: An Environmental Atlas and Resource Book</i>: "The largest Great Lakes fish harvests were recorded in 1889 and 1899 at some 67,000 tonnes (66,000 long tons; 74,000 short tons) [147 million pounds]."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glearb_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-glearb-63">[63]</a></sup>\n</p><p>By 1801, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Legislature" title="New York Legislature">New York Legislature</a> found it necessary to pass regulations curtailing obstructions to the natural migrations of <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_salmon" title="Atlantic salmon">Atlantic salmon</a> from Lake Erie into their spawning channels. In the early 19th century, the government of <a href="/wiki/Upper_Canada" title="Upper Canada">Upper Canada</a> found it necessary to introduce similar legislation prohibiting the use of weirs and nets at the mouths of Lake Ontario\'s tributaries. Other protective legislation was passed, as well, but enforcement remained difficult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bogue2001m_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bogue2001m-64">[64]</a></sup>\n</p><p>On both sides of the Canada&#8211;United States border, the proliferation of <a href="/wiki/Dam" title="Dam">dams</a> and impoundments have multiplied, necessitating more regulatory efforts. Concerns by the mid-19th century included obstructions in the rivers which prevented <a href="/wiki/Salmon" title="Salmon">salmon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lake_sturgeon" title="Lake sturgeon">lake sturgeon</a> from reaching their spawning grounds. The Wisconsin Fisheries Commission noted a reduction of roughly 25% in general fish harvests by 1875. The states have removed dams from rivers where necessary.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (September 2012)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Commission_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-Commission-65">[65]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Overfishing has been cited as a possible reason for a decrease in population of various <a href="/wiki/Freshwater_whitefish" title="Freshwater whitefish">whitefish</a>, important because of their culinary desirability and, hence, economic consequence. Moreover, between 1879 and 1899, reported whitefish harvests declined from some 24.3 million pounds (11 million&#160;kg) to just over 9 million pounds (4 million&#160;kg).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacdonaldService2009_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-MacdonaldService2009-66">[66]</a></sup> By 1900, commercial fishermen on Lake Michigan were hauling in an average of 41 million pounds of fish annually.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-js2_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-js2-67">[67]</a></sup> By 1938, Wisconsin\'s commercial fishing operations were motorized and mechanized, generating jobs for more than 2,000 workers, and hauling 14 million pounds per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-js2_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-js2-67">[67]</a></sup> The population of giant freshwater mussels was eliminated as the mussels were harvested for use as buttons by early Great Lakes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Entrepreneur" title="Entrepreneur">entrepreneurs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MacdonaldService2009_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-MacdonaldService2009-66">[66]</a></sup> Since 2000, the invasive <a href="/wiki/Quagga_mussel" title="Quagga mussel">quagga mussel</a> has smothered the bottom of Lake Michigan almost from shore to shore, and their numbers are estimated at 900 trillion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-js2_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-js2-67">[67]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The influx of parasitic <a href="/wiki/Lamprey" title="Lamprey">lamprey</a> populations after the development of the Erie Canal and the much later <a href="/wiki/Welland_Canal" title="Welland Canal">Welland Canal</a> led to the two federal governments of the US and Canada working on joint proposals to control it. By the mid-1950s, the lake trout populations of Lakes Michigan and Huron were reduced, with the lamprey deemed largely to blame. This led to the launch of the bi-national <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_Fishery_Commission" title="Great Lakes Fishery Commission">Great Lakes Fishery Commission</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lake_Superior_North_Shore(v2).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="975" data-file-width="1476" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Lake_Superior_North_Shore%28v2%29.jpg/220px-Lake_Superior_North_Shore%28v2%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Lake_Superior_North_Shore%28v2%29.jpg/330px-Lake_Superior_North_Shore%28v2%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Lake_Superior_North_Shore%28v2%29.jpg/440px-Lake_Superior_North_Shore%28v2%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lake_Superior_North_Shore(v2).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Cliffs at <a href="/wiki/Palisade_Head" title="Palisade Head">Palisade Head</a> on <a href="/wiki/Lake_Superior" title="Lake Superior">Lake Superior</a> in Minnesota near Silver Bay.</div></div></div>\n<p><i>The Great Lakes: An Environmental Atlas and Resource Book</i> (1972) noted: "Only pockets remain of the once large commercial fishery."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glearb_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-glearb-63">[63]</a></sup> But, water quality improvements realized during the 1970s and 1980s, combined with successful salmonid stocking programs, have enabled the growth of a large recreational fishery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Agency1998_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Agency1998-68">[68]</a></sup> The last commercial fisherman left Milwaukee in 2011 because of overfishing and anthropogenic changes to the biosphere.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-js2_67-3"><a href="#cite_note-js2-67">[67]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Since the 19th century an estimated 160 new species have found their way into the Great Lakes ecosystem; many have become invasive; the overseas ship ballast and ship hull parasitism are causing severe economic and ecological impacts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISEA_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-ISEA-70">[70]</a></sup> According to the Inland Seas Education Association, on average a new species enters the Great Lakes every eight months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISEA_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-ISEA-70">[70]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zebra_mussel_GLERL_3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="533" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Zebra_mussel_GLERL_3.jpg/170px-Zebra_mussel_GLERL_3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Zebra_mussel_GLERL_3.jpg/255px-Zebra_mussel_GLERL_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Zebra_mussel_GLERL_3.jpg/340px-Zebra_mussel_GLERL_3.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zebra_mussel_GLERL_3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Zebra_mussel" title="Zebra mussel">zebra mussel</a>-encrusted vector-averaging current meter from Lake Michigan.</div></div></div>\n<p>Introductions into the Great Lakes include the <a href="/wiki/Zebra_mussel" title="Zebra mussel">zebra mussel</a>, which was first discovered in 1988, and <a href="/wiki/Quagga_mussel" title="Quagga mussel">quagga mussel</a> in 1989. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusk" title="Mollusk">mollusks</a> are efficient filter feeders, competing with native <a href="/wiki/Mussel" title="Mussel">mussels</a> and reducing available food and spawning grounds for fish. In addition, the mussels may be a nuisance to industries by clogging pipes. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Fish_and_Wildlife_Service" title="U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service">U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service</a> estimates that the economic impact of the zebra mussel could be about $5&#160;billion over the next decade.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Alewife" title="Alewife">alewife</a> first entered the system west of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Ontario" title="Lake Ontario">Lake Ontario</a> via 19th-century canals. By the 1960s, the small silver fish had become a familiar nuisance to beach goers across Lakes Michigan, Huron, and Erie. Periodic mass dieoffs result in vast numbers of the fish washing up on shore; estimates by various governments have placed the percentage of Lake Michigan\'s biomass, which was made up of alewives in the early 1960s, as high as 90%. In the late 1960s, the various state and federal governments began stocking several species of salmonids, including the native <a href="/wiki/Lake_trout" title="Lake trout">lake trout</a> as well as non-native chinook and coho <a href="/wiki/Salmon" title="Salmon">salmon</a>; by the 1980s, alewife populations had dropped drastically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ruffe" title="Ruffe">ruffe</a>, a small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Percid" title="Percid">percid</a> fish from Eurasia, became the most abundant fish species in Lake Superior\'s <a href="/wiki/Saint_Louis_River" title="Saint Louis River">Saint Louis River</a> within five years of its detection in 1986. Its range, which has expanded to Lake Huron, poses a significant threat to the lower lake fishery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Five years after first being observed in the St. Clair River, the <a href="/wiki/Round_goby" title="Round goby">round goby</a> can now be found in all of the Great Lakes. The goby is considered undesirable for several reasons: it preys upon bottom-feeding fish, overruns optimal habitat, spawns multiple times a season, and can survive poor water quality conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Several species of exotic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_flea" title="Water flea">water fleas</a> have accidentally been introduced into the Great Lakes, such as the spiny waterflea, <i><a href="/wiki/Bythotrephes_longimanus" title="Bythotrephes longimanus">Bythotrephes longimanus</a></i>, and the fishhook waterflea, <i><a href="/wiki/Cercopagis_pengoi" title="Cercopagis pengoi">Cercopagis pengoi</a></i>, potentially having an effect on the <a href="/wiki/Zooplankton" title="Zooplankton">zooplankton</a> population. Several species of <a href="/wiki/Crayfish" title="Crayfish">crayfish</a> have also been introduced that may contend with native crayfish populations. More recently an electric fence has been set up across the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Sanitary_and_Ship_Canal" title="Chicago Sanitary and Ship Canal">Chicago Sanitary and Ship Canal</a> in order to keep several species of invasive <a href="/wiki/Asian_carp" title="Asian carp">Asian carp</a> out of the area. These fast-growing planktivorous fish have heavily colonized the Mississippi and Illinois river systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sea_lamprey" title="Sea lamprey">sea lamprey</a>, which has been particularly damaging to the native <a href="/wiki/Lake_trout" title="Lake trout">lake trout</a> population, is another example of a marine invasive species in the Great Lakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Invasive species, particularly zebra and quagga mussels, may be at least partially responsible for the collapse of the deepwater demersal fish community in Lake Huron,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> as well as drastic unprecedented changes in the zooplankton community of the lake.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flora">Flora</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Flora">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<ul><li><i>See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Flora_of_the_Great_Lakes_region_(North_America)" title="Category:Flora of the Great Lakes region (North America)">Flora of the Great Lakes region</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Category:Trees_of_the_Great_Lakes_region_(North_America)" title="Category:Trees of the Great Lakes region (North America)">Index: Trees of the Great Lakes region</a><i>.</i></li></ul>\n<p>Native <a href="/wiki/Habitat" title="Habitat">habitats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ecoregion" title="Ecoregion">ecoregions</a> in the Great Lakes region include:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Eastern_forest-boreal_transition" title="Eastern forest-boreal transition">Eastern forest-boreal transition</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Eastern_Great_Lakes_lowland_forests" title="Eastern Great Lakes lowland forests">Eastern Great Lakes lowland forests</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Southern_Great_Lakes_forests" title="Southern Great Lakes forests">Southern Great Lakes forests</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Central_forest-grasslands_transition" title="Central forest-grasslands transition">Central forest-grasslands transition</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Upper_Midwest_forest-savanna_transition" title="Upper Midwest forest-savanna transition">Upper Midwest forest-savanna transition</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Western_Great_Lakes_forests" title="Western Great Lakes forests">Western Great Lakes forests</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Central_Canadian_Shield_forests" title="Central Canadian Shield forests">Central Canadian Shield forests</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Laurentian_Mixed_Forest_Province" title="Laurentian Mixed Forest Province">Laurentian Mixed Forest Province</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Beech-maple_forest" title="Beech-maple forest">Beech-maple forest</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Habitats_of_the_Indiana_Dunes" title="Habitats of the Indiana Dunes">Habitats of the Indiana Dunes</a></li></ul>\n<p>Plant lists include:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/List_of_Michigan_flowers" title="List of Michigan flowers">List of Michigan flowers</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Minnesota_wild_flowers" title="List of Minnesota wild flowers">List of Minnesota wild flowers</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Minnesota_trees" title="List of Minnesota trees">List of Minnesota trees</a></li></ul>\n<p><b>Logging</b>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Logging" title="Logging">Logging</a> of the extensive forests in the Great Lakes region removed <a href="/wiki/Riparian_zone" title="Riparian zone">riparian</a> and adjacent tree cover over rivers and streams, which provide shade, moderating water temperatures in fish spawning grounds. Removal of trees also destabilized the soil, with greater volumes washed into stream beds causing siltation of gravel beds, and more frequent flooding.\n</p><p>Running cut logs down the tributary rivers into the Great Lakes also dislocated sediments. In 1884, the New York Fish Commission determined that the dumping of sawmill waste (chips and sawdust) had impacted fish populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dempsey2004_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dempsey2004-79">[79]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pollution">Pollution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Pollution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The first U.S. <a href="/wiki/Clean_Water_Act" title="Clean Water Act">Clean Water Act</a>, passed by a Congressional override after being vetoed by US President <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> in 1972, was a key piece of legislation,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> along with the bi-national <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_Water_Quality_Agreement" title="Great Lakes Water Quality Agreement">Great Lakes Water Quality Agreement</a> signed by Canada and the U.S. A variety of steps taken to process industrial and municipal pollution discharges into the system greatly improved water quality by the 1980s, and Lake Erie in particular is significantly cleaner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Discharge of toxic substances has been sharply reduced. Federal and state regulations control substances like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PCBs" title="PCBs">PCBs</a>.
