The first of 43 "<a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_Areas_of_Concern" title="Great Lakes Areas of Concern">Great Lakes Areas of Concern</a>" to be formally "de-listed" due to successful cleanup was Ontario\'s Collingwood Harbour in 1994; Ontario\'s Severn Sound followed in 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Presque_Isle_Bay" title="Presque Isle Bay">Presque Isle Bay</a> in Pennsylvania is formally listed as in recovery, as is Ontario\'s Spanish Harbour. Dozens of other Areas of Concern have received partial cleanups such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rouge_River_(Michigan)" title="Rouge River (Michigan)">Rouge River (Michigan)</a> and Waukegan Harbor (Illinois).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Phosphate detergents were historically a major source of nutrient to the Great Lakes algae blooms in particular in the warmer and shallower portions of the system such as <a href="/wiki/Lake_Erie" title="Lake Erie">Lake Erie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saginaw_Bay" title="Saginaw Bay">Saginaw Bay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Green_Bay_(Lake_Michigan)" title="Green Bay (Lake Michigan)">Green Bay</a>, and the southernmost portion of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Michigan" title="Lake Michigan">Lake Michigan</a>. By the mid-1980s, most jurisdictions bordering the Great Lakes had controlled phosphate detergents,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> resulting in sharp reductions in the frequency and extent of the blooms.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>In 2013, news of a garbage patch of <a href="/wiki/Plastic_pollution" title="Plastic pollution">plastic pollution</a> in the lakes was reported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mercury">Mercury</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Mercury">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Until 1970, <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a> was not listed as a harmful chemical, according to the United States Federal Water Quality Administration. Within the past ten years mercury has become more apparent in water tests. Mercury compounds have been used in paper mills to prevent slime from forming during their production, and chemical companies have used mercury to separate chlorine from brine solutions. Studies conducted by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">Environmental Protection Agency</a> have shown that when the mercury comes in contact with many of the bacteria and compounds in the fresh water, it forms the compound <a href="/wiki/Methylmercury" title="Methylmercury">methyl mercury</a>, which has a much greater impact on human health than elemental mercury due to a higher propensity for absorption. This form of mercury is not detrimental to a majority of fish types, but is very detrimental to people and other wildlife animals who consume the fish. Mercury has been known for health related problems such as birth defects in humans and animals, and the near extinction of eagles in the Great Lakes region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sewage">Sewage</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Sewage">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>The amount of raw sewage dumped into the waters was the primary focus of both the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_Water_Quality_Agreement" title="Great Lakes Water Quality Agreement">Great Lakes Water Quality Agreement</a> and federal laws passed in both countries during the 1970s. Implementation of secondary treatment of municipal sewage by major cities greatly reduced the routine discharge of untreated sewage during the 1970s and 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/International_Joint_Commission" title="International Joint Commission">International Joint Commission</a> in 2009 summarized the change: "Since the early 1970s, the level of treatment to reduce pollution from waste water discharges to the Great Lakes has improved considerably. This is a result of significant expenditures to date on both infrastructure and technology, and robust regulatory systems that have proven to be, on the whole, quite effective."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ijc.org_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-ijc.org-88">[88]</a></sup> The commission reported that all urban sewage treatment systems on the U.S. side of the lakes had implemented secondary treatment, as had all on the Canadian side except for five small systems.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>However contrary to federal laws in both countries, those treatment system upgrades have not yet eliminated <a href="/wiki/Combined_sewer" title="Combined sewer">Combined sewer</a> Overflow events.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This describes when older sewerage systems, which combine storm water with sewage into single sewers heading to the treatment plant, are temporarily overwhelmed by heavy rainstorms. Local sewage treatment authorities then must release untreated effluent, a mix of rainwater and sewage, into local water bodies. While enormous public investments such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_Tunnel" title="Deep Tunnel">Deep Tunnel</a> projects in <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a> and <a href="/wiki/Milwaukee" title="Milwaukee">Milwaukee</a> have greatly reduced the frequency and volume of these events, they have not been eliminated. The number of such overflow events in Ontario, for example, is flat according to the International Joint Commission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ijc.org_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-ijc.org-88">[88]</a></sup> Reports about this issue on the U.S. side highlight five large municipal systems (those of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Detroit,_Michigan" title="Detroit, Michigan">Detroit</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleveland,_Ohio" title="Cleveland, Ohio">Cleveland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buffalo,_New_York" title="Buffalo, New York">Buffalo</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milwaukee,_Wisconsin" title="Milwaukee, Wisconsin">Milwaukee</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gary,_Indiana" title="Gary, Indiana">Gary</a>) as being the largest current periodic sources of untreated discharges into the Great Lakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diatomshapes.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="256" data-file-width="256" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Diatomshapes.jpg/220px-Diatomshapes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/78/Diatomshapes.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diatomshapes.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Different Diatom Shapes</div></div></div> <div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diatoms_through_the_microscope.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1180" data-file-width="1796" decoding="async" height="145" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Diatoms_through_the_microscope.jpg/220px-Diatoms_through_the_microscope.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Diatoms_through_the_microscope.jpg/330px-Diatoms_through_the_microscope.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Diatoms_through_the_microscope.jpg/440px-Diatoms_through_the_microscope.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diatoms_through_the_microscope.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diatoms seen through the microscope. These minuscule <a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">phytoplankton</a> are encased within a <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicate</a> <a href="/wiki/Cell_wall" title="Cell wall">cell wall</a>.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Impacts_of_climate_change_on_algae">Impacts of climate change on algae</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Impacts of climate change on algae">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Algae such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diatoms" title="Diatoms">diatoms</a>, along with other <a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">phytoplankton</a>, are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Photosynthetic" title="Photosynthetic">photosynthetic</a> primary producers supporting the <a href="/wiki/Food_web" title="Food web">food web</a> of The Great Lakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> The changes in the size or in the function of the primary producers may have a direct or an indirect impact on the food web. Photosynthesis carried out by diatoms comprises about one fifth of the total photosynthesis. By taking CO2 out of the water, to photosynthesize, diatoms help to stabilize the pH of the water, as otherwise CO2 would react with water making it more acidic. Diatoms acquire inorganic carbon thought passive diffusion of CO2 and HCO3, as well they use Carbonic Anhydrase mediated active transport to speed up this process (1<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>). Large diatoms require more carbon uptake than smaller diatoms (8<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-92">[92]</a></sup>). There is a positive correlation between the surface area and the chlorophyll concentration of diatom cells (2<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>).\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Le-griffon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="602" data-file-width="735" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Le-griffon.jpg/220px-Le-griffon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Le-griffon.jpg/330px-Le-griffon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Le-griffon.jpg/440px-Le-griffon.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Le-griffon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A woodcut of <i>Le Griffon</i></div></div></div>\n<p>Several Native American tribes inhabited the region since at least 10,000 BC, after the end of the <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin_glaciation" title="Wisconsin glaciation">Wisconsin glaciation</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The peoples of the Great Lakes traded with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hopewell_culture" title="Hopewell culture">Hopewell culture</a> from around 1000 AD, as copper nuggets have been extracted from the region, and fashioned into ornaments and weapons in the mounds of Southern Ohio. The brigantine <i><a href="/wiki/Le_Griffon" title="Le Griffon">Le Griffon</a></i>, which was commissioned by <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9-Robert_Cavelier,_Sieur_de_La_Salle" title="Ren&#233;-Robert Cavelier, Sieur de La Salle">Ren&#233;-Robert Cavelier, Sieur de La Salle</a>, was built at Cayuga Creek, near the southern end of the <a href="/wiki/Niagara_River" title="Niagara River">Niagara River</a>, and became the first known sailing ship to travel the upper Great Lakes on August 7, 1679.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Woodford1991_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Woodford1991-94">[94]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Rush%E2%80%93Bagot_Treaty" title="Rush&#8211;Bagot Treaty">Rush&#8211;Bagot Treaty</a> signed in 1818, after the <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">War of 1812</a> and the later <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Washington_(1871)" title="Treaty of Washington (1871)">Treaty of Washington</a> eventually led to a complete disarmament of naval vessels in the Great Lakes. Nonetheless, both nations maintain coast guard vessels in the Great Lakes.\n</p><p>During settlement, the Great Lakes and its rivers were the only practical means of moving people and freight. <a href="/wiki/Barge" title="Barge">Barges</a> from middle North America were able to reach the Atlantic Ocean from the Great Lakes when the Welland canal opened in 1824 and the later <a href="/wiki/Erie_Canal" title="Erie Canal">Erie Canal</a> opened in 1825.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bernstein2010oi_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bernstein2010oi-95">[95]</a></sup> By 1848, with the opening of the <a href="/wiki/Illinois_and_Michigan_Canal" title="Illinois and Michigan Canal">Illinois and Michigan Canal</a> at <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>, direct access to the <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi River</a> was possible from the lakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Danzer2011re_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Danzer2011re-96">[96]</a></sup> With these two canals an all-inland water route was provided between <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans" title="New Orleans">New Orleans</a>.\n</p><p>The main business of many of the passenger lines in the 19th century was transporting <a href="/wiki/Immigration" title="Immigration">immigrants</a>. Many of the larger cities owe their existence to their position on the lakes as a freight destination as well as for being a magnet for immigrants. After railroads and surface roads developed, the freight and passenger businesses dwindled and, except for ferries and a few foreign cruise ships, has now vanished.\nThe immigration routes still have an effect today. Immigrants often formed their own communities and some areas have a pronounced ethnicity, such as Dutch, German, Polish, Finnish, and many others. Since many immigrants settled for a time in New England before moving westward, many areas on the U.S. side of the Great Lakes also have a New England feel, especially in home styles and accent.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eastland_Christopher_Columbus_leaving_Chicago_ca1912.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="250" data-file-width="396" decoding="async" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Eastland_Christopher_Columbus_leaving_Chicago_ca1912.jpg/220px-Eastland_Christopher_Columbus_leaving_Chicago_ca1912.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Eastland_Christopher_Columbus_leaving_Chicago_ca1912.jpg/330px-Eastland_Christopher_Columbus_leaving_Chicago_ca1912.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/39/Eastland_Christopher_Columbus_leaving_Chicago_ca1912.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eastland_Christopher_Columbus_leaving_Chicago_ca1912.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a href="/wiki/SS_Eastland" title="SS Eastland">Eastland</a></i> leaving Chicago, c. 1909</div></div></div>\n<p>Since general freight these days is transported by railroads and trucks, domestic ships mostly move bulk cargoes, such as <a href="/wiki/Iron_ore" title="Iron ore">iron ore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Limestone" title="Limestone">limestone</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> industry. The domestic bulk freight developed because of the nearby mines. It was more economical to transport the ingredients for steel to centralized plants rather than try to make steel on the spot. Grain exports are also a major cargo on the lakes.\n</p><p>In the 19th century and early 20th centuries, iron and other ores such as copper were shipped south on (downbound ships), and supplies, food, and coal were shipped north (upbound). Because of the location of the coal fields in Pennsylvania and <a href="/wiki/West_Virginia" title="West Virginia">West Virginia</a>, and the general northeast track of the <a href="/wiki/Appalachian_Mountains" title="Appalachian Mountains">Appalachian Mountains</a>, railroads naturally developed shipping routes that went due north to ports such as <a href="/wiki/Erie,_Pennsylvania" title="Erie, Pennsylvania">Erie, Pennsylvania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ashtabula,_Ohio" title="Ashtabula, Ohio">Ashtabula, Ohio</a>.\n</p><p>Because the lake maritime community largely developed independently, it has some distinctive vocabulary. Ships, no matter the size, are called <i>boats</i>. When the sailing ships gave way to steamships, they were called <i>steamboats</i>&#8212;the same term used on the Mississippi. The ships also have a distinctive design (see <a href="/wiki/Lake_freighter" title="Lake freighter">Lake freighter</a>). Ships that primarily trade on the lakes are known as <i><a href="/wiki/Lake_freighter" title="Lake freighter">lakers</a></i>. Foreign boats are known as <i>salties</i>. One of the more common sights on the lakes has been since about 1950 the 1,000&#8209;by&#8209;105-foot (305-by-32-meter), 78,850-long-ton (80,120-metric-ton) self-unloader. This is a laker with a conveyor belt system that can unload itself by swinging a crane over the side.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Today, the Great Lakes fleet is much smaller in numbers than it once was because of the increased use of overland freight, and a few larger ships replacing many small ones.\n</p><p>During World War II, the risk of submarine attacks against coastal training facilities motivated the United States Navy to operate two aircraft carriers on the Great Lakes, <a href="/wiki/USS_Sable_(IX-81)" title="USS Sable (IX-81)">USS&#160;<i>Sable</i>&#160;(IX-81)</a> and <a href="/wiki/USS_Wolverine_(IX-64)" title="USS Wolverine (IX-64)">USS&#160;<i>Wolverine</i>&#160;(IX-64)</a>. Both served as training ships to qualify naval aviators in carrier landing and takeoff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gonzalez2008o_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gonzalez2008o-98">[98]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Lake_Champlain" title="Lake Champlain">Lake Champlain</a> briefly became the sixth Great Lake of the United States on March 6, 1998, when <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">President Clinton</a> signed Senate Bill 927. This bill, which reauthorized the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_Sea_Grant_Program" title="National Sea Grant Program">National Sea Grant Program</a>, contained a line declaring Lake Champlain to be a Great Lake. Not coincidentally, this status allows neighboring states to apply for additional federal research and education funds allocated to these national resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> Following a small uproar, the Senate voted to revoke the designation on March 24 (although New York and Vermont universities would continue to receive funds to monitor and study the lake).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the early years of the 21st century, water levels in the Great Lakes were a concern.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Researchers at the <a href="/wiki/Mowat_Centre" title="Mowat Centre">Mowat Centre</a> said that low levels could cost $19bn by 2050.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Overview_of_the_Great_Lakes_from_orbit.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Overview_of_the_Great_Lakes_from_orbit.jpg/290px-Overview_of_the_Great_Lakes_from_orbit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Overview_of_the_Great_Lakes_from_orbit.jpg/435px-Overview_of_the_Great_Lakes_from_orbit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Overview_of_the_Great_Lakes_from_orbit.jpg/580px-Overview_of_the_Great_Lakes_from_orbit.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Overview_of_the_Great_Lakes_from_orbit.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Photograph of Lakes Ontario, Erie and Huron plus the <a href="/wiki/Finger_Lakes" title="Finger Lakes">Finger Lakes</a> of upstate New York, June 14, 2012, taken aboard the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a>, with lake names added</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shipping">Shipping</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Shipping">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Except when the water is frozen during winter, more than 100 lake freighters operate continuously on the Great Lakes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clui_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-clui-103">[103]</a></sup> which remain a major <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_transport" title="Water transport">water transport</a> corridor for bulk goods. The <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_Waterway" title="Great Lakes Waterway">Great Lakes Waterway</a> connects all the lakes; the smaller <a href="/wiki/Saint_Lawrence_Seaway" title="Saint Lawrence Seaway">Saint Lawrence Seaway</a> connects the lakes to the Atlantic oceans. Some <a href="/wiki/Lake_freighter" title="Lake freighter">lake freighters</a> are too large to use the Seaway, and only operate on the Waterway and lakes.\n</p><p>In 2002, 162 million net <a href="/wiki/Ton" title="Ton">tons</a> of dry bulk  cargo were moved on the Lakes. This was, in order of volume: iron ore, grain and <a href="/wiki/Potash" title="Potash">potash</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> The iron ore and much of the stone and coal are used in the steel industry. There is also some shipping of liquid and containerized cargo but most container ships cannot pass the locks on the Saint Lawrence Seaway because the ships are too wide.\n</p><p>Only four bridges are on the Great Lakes other than Lake Ontario because of the cost of building structures high enough for ships to pass under. The <a href="/wiki/Blue_Water_Bridge" title="Blue Water Bridge">Blue Water Bridge</a> is, for example, more than 150 feet high and more than a mile long.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clui_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-clui-103">[103]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Drinking_water_and_compact">Drinking water and compact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Drinking water and compact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_Compact" title="Great Lakes Compact">Great Lakes Compact</a></div>\n<p>The Great Lakes are used to supply drinking water to tens of millions of people in bordering areas. This valuable resource is collectively administered by the state and provincial governments adjacent to the lakes, who have agreed to the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_Compact" title="Great Lakes Compact">Great Lakes Compact</a> to regulate water supply and use.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recreation">Recreation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Recreation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marina_Fest.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Marina_Fest.jpg/220px-Marina_Fest.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Marina_Fest.jpg/330px-Marina_Fest.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Marina_Fest.jpg/440px-Marina_Fest.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marina_Fest.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Escanaba,_Michigan" title="Escanaba, Michigan">Escanaba</a>\'s Ludington Park in Michigan</div></div></div>\n<p>Tourism and recreation are major industries on the Great Lakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GroverKrantzberg2012_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-GroverKrantzberg2012-105">[105]</a></sup> A few small cruise ships operate on the Great Lakes including a couple of <a href="/wiki/Sailing" title="Sailing">sailing ships</a>. Sport fishing, commercial fishing, and Native American fishing represent a U.S.$4 billion a year industry with <a href="/wiki/Salmon" title="Salmon">salmon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coregonus" title="Coregonus">whitefish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smelt_(fish)" title="Smelt (fish)">smelt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lake_trout" title="Lake trout">lake trout</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bass_(fish)" title="Bass (fish)">bass</a> and <a href="/wiki/Walleye" title="Walleye">walleye</a> being major catches. Many other <a href="/wiki/List_of_water_sports" title="List of water sports">water sports</a> are practiced on the lakes such as <a href="/wiki/Yachting" title="Yachting">yachting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sea_kayak" title="Sea kayak">sea kayaking</a>, <a href="/wiki/Recreational_diving" title="Recreational diving">diving</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kitesurfing" title="Kitesurfing">kitesurfing</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Powerboat" title="Powerboat">powerboating</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lake_surfing" title="Lake surfing">lake surfing</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_Circle_Tour" title="Great Lakes Circle Tour">Great Lakes Circle Tour</a> is a designated scenic road system connecting all of the Great Lakes and the Saint Lawrence River.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Lakes_passenger_steamers">Great Lakes passenger steamers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Great Lakes passenger steamers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_passenger_steamers" title="Great Lakes passenger steamers">Great Lakes passenger steamers</a></div>\n<p>From 1844 through 1857, <a href="/wiki/Palace_steamer" title="Palace steamer">palace steamers</a> carried passengers and cargo around the Great Lakes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson1991_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thompson1991-107">[107]</a></sup> In the first half of the 20th century large luxurious passenger steamers sailed the lakes in opulence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KoutskyKoutsky2006_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-KoutskyKoutsky2006-108">[108]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Detroit_and_Cleveland_Navigation_Company" title="Detroit and Cleveland Navigation Company">Detroit and Cleveland Navigation Company</a> had several vessels at the time and hired workers from all walks of life to help operate these vessels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> Several ferries currently operate on the Great Lakes to carry passengers to various islands, including Isle Royale, Drummond Island, <a href="/wiki/Pelee,_Ontario" title="Pelee, Ontario">Pelee Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mackinac_Island" title="Mackinac Island">Mackinac Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beaver_Island_(Lake_Michigan)" title="Beaver Island (Lake Michigan)">Beaver Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bois_Blanc_Island_(Ontario)" title="Bois Blanc Island (Ontario)">Bois Blanc Island (Ontario)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bois_Blanc_Island_(Michigan)" title="Bois Blanc Island (Michigan)">Bois Blanc Island (Michigan)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kelleys_Island,_Ohio" title="Kelleys Island, Ohio">Kelleys Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Bass_Island" title="South Bass Island">South Bass Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Manitou_Island" title="North Manitou Island">North Manitou Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Manitou_Island" title="South Manitou Island">South Manitou Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harsens_Island" title="Harsens Island">Harsens Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manitoulin_Island" title="Manitoulin Island">Manitoulin Island</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Toronto_Islands" title="Toronto Islands">Toronto Islands</a>. As of 2007, four car ferry services cross the Great Lakes, two on Lake Michigan: a steamer from <a href="/wiki/Ludington,_Michigan" title="Ludington, Michigan">Ludington, Michigan</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Manitowoc,_Wisconsin" title="Manitowoc, Wisconsin">Manitowoc, Wisconsin</a>, and a high speed catamaran from <a href="/wiki/Milwaukee" title="Milwaukee">Milwaukee</a> to <a href="/wiki/Muskegon,_Michigan" title="Muskegon, Michigan">Muskegon, Michigan</a>, one on Lake Erie: a boat from <a href="/wiki/Kingsville,_Ontario" title="Kingsville, Ontario">Kingsville, Ontario</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Leamington,_Ontario" title="Leamington, Ontario">Leamington, Ontario</a>, to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pelee_Island,_Ontario" title="Pelee Island, Ontario">Pelee Island, Ontario</a>, then onto <a href="/wiki/Sandusky,_Ohio" title="Sandusky, Ohio">Sandusky, Ohio</a>, and one on Lake Huron: the M.S. <i>Chi-Cheemaun</i> <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> runs between Tobermory and South Baymouth, Manitoulin Island, operated by the Owen Sound Transportation Company. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spirit_of_Ontario_I" title="Spirit of Ontario I">An international ferry</a> across Lake Ontario <a href="/wiki/Rochester,_New_York" title="Rochester, New York">Rochester, New York</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a> ran during 2004 and 2005, but is no longer in operation.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shipwrecks">Shipwrecks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Shipwrecks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The large size of the Great Lakes increases the risk of water travel; <a href="/wiki/Storm" title="Storm">storms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Reef" title="Reef">reefs</a> are common threats. The lakes are prone to sudden and severe storms, in particular in the autumn, from late October until early December. Hundreds of ships have met their end on the lakes. The greatest concentration of shipwrecks lies near <a href="/wiki/Thunder_Bay_(Michigan)" title="Thunder Bay (Michigan)">Thunder Bay (Michigan)</a>, beneath Lake Huron, near the point where eastbound and westbound shipping lanes converge.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Lake_Superior" title="Lake Superior">Lake Superior</a> shipwreck coast from <a href="/wiki/Grand_Marais,_Michigan" title="Grand Marais, Michigan">Grand Marais, Michigan</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Whitefish_Point" title="Whitefish Point">Whitefish Point</a> became known as the "Graveyard of the Great Lakes". More vessels have been lost in the Whitefish Point area than any other part of Lake Superior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Whitefish_Point_Underwater_Preserve" title="Whitefish Point Underwater Preserve">Whitefish Point Underwater Preserve</a> serves as an underwater museum to protect the many shipwrecks in this area.\n</p><p>The first ship to sink in Lake Michigan was <i><a href="/wiki/Le_Griffon" title="Le Griffon">Le Griffon</a></i>, also the first ship to sail the Great Lakes. Caught in a 1679 storm while trading furs between Green Bay and Michilimacinac, she was lost with all hands aboard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ledger041104_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-ledger041104-112">[112]</a></sup> Its wreck may have been found in 2004,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> but a wreck subsequently discovered in a different location was also claimed in 2014 to be <i>Le Griffon</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The largest and last major freighter wrecked on the lakes was the <a href="/wiki/SS_Edmund_Fitzgerald" title="SS Edmund Fitzgerald">SS&#160;<i>Edmund Fitzgerald</i></a>, which sank on November 10, 1975, just over 17 miles (30&#160;km) offshore from Whitefish Point on Lake Superior. The largest loss of life in a shipwreck out on the lakes may have been that of <a href="/wiki/PS_Lady_Elgin" title="PS Lady Elgin"><i>Lady Elgin</i></a>, wrecked in 1860 with the loss of around 400 lives on Lake Michigan. In an incident at a Chicago dock in 1915, the <a href="/wiki/SS_Eastland" title="SS Eastland">SS&#160;<i>Eastland</i></a> rolled over while loading passengers, killing 841.\n</p><p>In August 2007, the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_Shipwreck_Museum" title="Great Lakes Shipwreck Museum">Great Lakes Shipwreck Historical Society</a> announced that it had found the wreckage of <i>Cyprus</i>, a 420-foot (130&#160;m) long, century-old <a href="/wiki/Ore" title="Ore">ore</a> carrier. <i>Cyprus</i> sank during a Lake Superior storm on October 11, 1907, during its second voyage while hauling iron ore from <a href="/wiki/Superior,_Wisconsin" title="Superior, Wisconsin">Superior, Wisconsin</a>, to <a href="/wiki/Buffalo,_New_York" title="Buffalo, New York">Buffalo, New York</a>. The entire crew of 23 drowned, except one, Charles Pitz, who floated on a life raft for almost seven hours.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In June 2008, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deep_sea_diving" title="Deep sea diving">deep sea divers</a> in Lake Ontario found the wreck of the 1780 <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">Royal Navy</a> warship <a href="/wiki/HMS_Ontario_(1780)" title="HMS Ontario (1780)">HMS&#160;<i>Ontario</i></a> in what has been described as an "archaeological miracle".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> There are no plans to raise her as the site is being treated as a war grave.\n</p><p>In June 2010, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L.R._Doty" title="L.R. Doty">L.R. Doty</a></i> was found in Lake Michigan by an exploration diving team led by dive boat Captain Jitka Hanakova from her boat the Molly V.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> The ship sank in October 1898, probably attempting to rescue a small schooner, <i>Olive Jeanette</i>, during a terrible storm.\n</p><p>Still missing are the two last warships to sink in the Great Lakes, the French minesweepers, <a href="/wiki/French_minesweepers_Inkerman_and_Cerisoles" title="French minesweepers Inkerman and Cerisoles"><i>Inkerman</i> and <i>Cerisoles</i></a>, which vanished in Lake Superior during a blizzard in 1918. 78 lives were lost making it the largest loss of life in Lake Superior and the greatest unexplained loss of life in the Great Lakes.\n</p><p><b>Related articles</b>\n</p>\n<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/List_of_shipwrecks_in_the_Great_Lakes" title="List of shipwrecks in the Great Lakes">List of shipwrecks in the Great Lakes</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Category:Shipwrecks_in_the_Great_Lakes" title="Category:Shipwrecks in the Great Lakes">Index: Shipwrecks in the Great Lakes</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Storms_of_the_North_American_Great_Lakes" title="Great Storms of the North American Great Lakes">Great Storms of the North American Great Lakes</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_Storm_of_1913" title="Great Lakes Storm of 1913">Great Lakes Storm of 1913</a></li></ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legislation">Legislation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Legislation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_Lakes_1.PNG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1243" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="249" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Great_Lakes_1.PNG/300px-Great_Lakes_1.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Great_Lakes_1.PNG/450px-Great_Lakes_1.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Great_Lakes_1.PNG/600px-Great_Lakes_1.PNG 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Great_Lakes_1.PNG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Various national, state, provincial, and municipal jurisdictions govern the Great Lakes</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1872, a treaty gave access to the St. Lawrence River to the United States, and access to Lake Michigan to the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Confederation" title="Canadian Confederation">Dominion of Canada</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/International_Joint_Commission" title="International Joint Commission">International Joint Commission</a> was established in 1909 to help prevent and resolve disputes relating to the use and quality of boundary waters, and to advise Canada and the United States on questions related to water resources. Concerns over diversion of Lake water are of concern to both Americans and Canadians. Some water is diverted through the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_River" title="Chicago River">Chicago River</a> to operate the <a href="/wiki/Illinois_Waterway" title="Illinois Waterway">Illinois Waterway</a> but the flow is limited by treaty. Possible schemes for bottled water plants and diversion to dry regions of the continent raise concerns. Under the U.S. "Water Resources Development Act",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> diversion of water from the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_Basin" title="Great Lakes Basin">Great Lakes Basin</a> requires the approval of all eight Great Lakes governors through the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_Commission" title="Great Lakes Commission">Great Lakes Commission</a>, which rarely occurs. International treaties regulate large diversions.\n</p><p>In 1998, the Canadian company Nova Group won approval from the Province of Ontario to withdraw 158,000,000 U.S. gallons (600,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) of Lake Superior water annually to ship by tanker to Asian countries. Public outcry forced the company to abandon the plan before it began. Since that time, the eight Great Lakes Governors and the Premiers of Ontario and Quebec have negotiated the Great Lakes-Saint Lawrence River Basin Sustainable Water Resources Agreement<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup> and the Great Lakes-St. Lawrence River Basin Water Resources Compact<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> that would prevent most future diversion proposals and all long-distance ones. The agreements strengthen protection against abusive water withdrawal practices within the Great Lakes basin. On December 13, 2005, the Governors and Premiers signed these two agreements, the first of which is between all ten jurisdictions. It is somewhat more detailed and protective, though its legal strength has not yet been tested in court. The second, the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes_Compact" title="Great Lakes Compact">Great Lakes Compact</a>, has been approved by the state legislatures of all eight states that border the Great Lakes as well as the U.S. Congress, and was signed into law by President <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> on October 3, 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Great Lakes Restoration Initiative, described as "the largest investment in the Great Lakes in two decades,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> was funded at $475 million in the U.S. federal government\'s Fiscal Year 2011 budget, and $300 million in the Fiscal Year 2012 budget. Through the program a coalition of federal agencies is making grants to local and state entities for toxics cleanups, wetlands and coastline restoration projects, and invasive species-related projects.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coast_Guard_live_fire_exercises">Coast Guard live fire exercises</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Great_Lakes&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Coast Guard live fire exercises">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In 2006, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Coast_Guard" title="United States Coast Guard">United States Coast Guard</a> (USCG) proposed a plan to designate 34 areas in the Great Lakes, at least 5&#160;mi (8&#160;km) offshore, as permanent safety zones for live fire machine gun practice. In August 2006, the plan was published in the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Register" title="Federal Register">Federal Register</a>. The USCG reserved the right to hold target practice whenever the weather allowed with a two-hour notice. These firing ranges would be open to the public when not in use. In response to requests from the public, the Coast Guard held a series of public meetings in nine U.S. cities to solicit comment. During these meetings many people voiced concerns about the plan and its impact on the environment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> On December 18, 2006, the Coast Guard announced its decision to withdraw the entire proposal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> Officials said they would look into alternative ammunition, modifying the proposed zones and have more public dialogue before proposing a new plan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
